Al-Karkhi on Generality: Report vs. Command
Al-Jassas reports and critiques al-Karkhi's view on generality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the reported view of Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi on generality and how al-Jassas evaluated its authenticity.

Core Idea
Al-Karkhi reportedly differentiated between reports and commands, but al-Jassas casts doubt on that report.

The passage continues with an anecdote from al-Jassas about a report from Abu al-Tayyib ibn Shihab concerning the view of Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi, a leading Hanafi jurist. According to the report, al-Karkhi said he stops (waqf) regarding the generality of reports (akhbar) but affirms generality in commands and prohibitions. Al-Jassas interprets this as al-Karkhi stopping regarding the threat (wa'id) against sinners. However, al-Jassas doubts the reliability of this report and asserts that al-Karkhi actually affirmed generality in all cases.

Guided Path
1
Read the anecdote
Read the passage about Abu al-Tayyib's report on al-Karkhi's view.
2
Understand al-Karkhi's reported distinction
Al-Karkhi reportedly said he stops (waqif) regarding generality of reports but affirms it in commands and prohibitions. This would imply he suspends judgment on the wa'id verses.
3
Consider al-Jassas's critique
Al-Jassas questions the narrator's reliability and states he never heard al-Karkhi make such a distinction; rather, he affirmed generality in all contexts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حتى إن أبا بكر الجصاص قال بعد ما استشهد لصحة قوله فبان بما وصفنا أن العموم من مفهوم لسان العرب، وأن ذلك من مذهب السلف من غير خلاف بينهم وما خالف من هذا أحد من السلف ومن بعدهم إلى أن نشأت فرقة من المرجئة ضاق عليهم المذهب في القول بالإرجاء فلجأت إلى دفع القول بالعموم رأسًا لئلا يلزمها لخصومها القول بوعيد الفساق بظاهر الآي المقتضية لذلك. هذا كله لفظه ذكره في كتابه المصنف في أصول الفقه وقال أيضا في أول هذا الباب وحكى لي أبو الطيب بن شهاب عن أبي الحسن الكرخي أنه قال : إني أقف في عموم الأخبار وأقول بالعموم في الأمر والنهي، فقلت لأبي الطيب فهذا يدل على أن مذهبه كان الوقف في وعيد فساق أهل الملة، فقال : هكذا كان مذهبه، قال الجصاص وأبو الطيب هذا غير مهم عندي فيما يحكيه، وقد جالس أبا سعيد البردعي وشيوخنا القدماء ولم أسمع أن أبا الحسن الكرخي يفرق بين الخير والأمر والنهي في ذلك بل كان يقول بالعموم والإطلاق.
Until Abu Bakr al-Jassas said, after he cited evidence for the correctness of his statement: 'So it became clear by what we described that generality is from the concept of the Arabic language, and that it is from the way of the Salaf without any disagreement among them, and no one from the Salaf or after them opposed this until a faction of the Murji'ah arose, and the doctrine of irja' became constrained for them, so they resorted to rejecting the statement of generality entirely, lest their opponents compel them to the statement of wa'id against sinners by the apparent verses necessitating that.' This is all his wording; he mentioned it in his book 'al-Musannaf fi Usul al-Fiqh'. And he also said at the beginning of this chapter: 'Abu al-Tayyib ibn Shihab narrated to me from Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi that he said: I stop (waqif) regarding the generality of reports, and I say generality in command and prohibition.' So I said to Abu al-Tayyib: 'This indicates that his school was stopping regarding the wa'id of sinful people of the religion.' He said: 'Thus was his school.' Al-Jassas said: 'And Abu al-Tayyib is not important to me in what he narrates. He accompanied Abu Sa'id al-Barda'i and our early shaykhs, and I did not hear that Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi differentiated between report and command and prohibition in that; rather he used to say generality and absoluteness.'
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage presents a direct quote from Abu Bakr al-Jassas. He argues that the principle of generality (al-'umum) is a well-established concept in the Arabic language and the way of the Salaf, without any early disagreement. The only opposition came from a faction of the Murji'ah, who found the doctrine of irja' (postponement of judgment) problematic because the apparent verses imply a definite threat (wa'id) against sinners. To avoid this conclusion, they rejected generality outright. Al-Jassas then reports an anecdote: Abu al-Tayyib ibn Shihab narrated that Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi said he stops (waqf) regarding the generality of reports (akhbar) but affirms generality in commands and prohibitions. Al-Jassas interprets this as al-Karkhi stopping regarding the wa'id of sinful believers. However, al-Jassas doubts this narration, noting that Abu al-Tayyib's reliability is not significant to him, and he never heard al-Karkhi differentiate between reports and commands; rather, al-Karkhi affirmed generality in all cases.

Key Terms
وقف
عموم
مرجئة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that al-Karkhi definitively held a distinction between reports and commands on generality.
Correction: Al-Jassas casts doubt on the report and asserts that al-Karkhi actually affirmed generality in all cases without differentiation.

Checkpoint
According to al-Jassas, what was the actual view of Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi on generality?
A) He stopped regarding reports but affirmed in commands.
B) He rejected generality entirely.
C) He affirmed generality in all cases without differentiation.
D) He only used generality in legal theory.

Key Takeaway
Al-Jassas questions the report that al-Karkhi differentiated between reports and commands on generality, asserting that al-Karkhi actually affirmed generality universally.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a claim about someone's beliefs, do you accept it at face value, or do you question the source? How does al-Jassas's skepticism challenge your own tendency to trust reports without verification?
?
Al-Jassas dismissed Abu al-Tayyib as 'not important' despite his association with shaykhs. Reflect on a time you trusted someone's authority because of their connections, only to find their report unreliable. How can you guard against such misplaced trust?
?
If you were in al-Karkhi's position and a student misrepresented your view, what steps would you take to correct it? How does this lesson compel you to be precise when conveying the beliefs of others, especially in matters of religion?

Al-Maturidi's Position on Generality and Delayed Specification
States al-Maturidi's view that general may intend specific and delayed proof is clarification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Maturidi's view that a general expression may intend a specific meaning, and that a delayed proof of specification is clarification (bayan), not abrogation (naskh).

Core Idea
General can intend specific; delayed proof clarifies, not abrogates.

Imam al-Maturidi and early Hanafi-Maturidi scholars held a distinctive position on the relationship between general and specific expressions. They taught that a general phrase can be uttered with the intention of a specific meaning, and that any delayed evidence of specification does not abrogate the original text but merely clarifies what was always intended. This lesson explores that view, its contrast with the Mu'tazili stance, and the later scholarly reactions within the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the key terms: 'umum (generality), khushus (specificity), bayan (clarification), naskh (abrogation).
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify al-Maturidi's two main claims
First, that generality may intend specificity. Second, that a delayed proof of specification (except for qualification) is bayan, not naskh.
3
Understand the distinction from Mu'tazila
Al-Maturidi considered the opposite view—that general always indicates generality and delayed specification is naskh—to be the Mu'tazili position.
4
Reflect on the historical context
Later Hanafis in the author's region initially rejected those who held the Mu'tazili view, but a scholar later emerged who adopted an Iraqi Hanafi position and attributed the original view to al-Shafi'i.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والشيخ أبو منصور الماتريدي وغيره من مشايخنا رحمهم الله كانوا يقولون إن العموم يذكر ويراد به الخصوص، وما يقوم من دليل الخصوص متأخر إلا دليل القيد فهو بيان المراد لا النسخ والصيغة المتعربة عن دليل الخصوص أو القيد ليست بدليل إرادة العموم والإطلاق ويعدون القول بذلك مذهب المعتزلة، وكان مشايخنا في ديارنا ينكرون على القائلين بذلك وينسبونهم إلى الاعتزال إلى أن نشأ فيهم من كان يميل في أصول الفقه إلى العراقيين من أصحابنا فأتبعهم في مسألة العموم وذكر ما تصوره عنده أنه دليل له في المسألة ونسب ما هو قول أصحابنا إلى الشافعي ولم يحم حول مسألة الوعيد، والله تعالى أعلم بعقيدته في ذلك.
And Shaykh Abu Mansur al-Maturidi and other of our mashayikh—may Allah have mercy on them—used to say that generality is mentioned and intended with it specificity, and whatever proof of specificity comes later—except the proof of qualification—it is clarification (bayan) of the intended meaning, not abrogation. And the formulation bare of a proof of specificity or qualification is not a proof of intending generality and absoluteness. And they considered the statement on that to be the school of the Mu'tazila. And our mashayikh in our region used to deny those who said that and attribute them to i'tizal, until there arose among them one who inclined in usul al-fiqh towards the Iraqis among our companions, so he followed them in the issue of generality and mentioned what he considered to be a proof for him in the issue, and he attributed what is the statement of our companions to al-Shafi'i, and he did not go around the issue of wa'id. And Allah Almighty knows best regarding his belief in that.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage presents the position of Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi and other senior Hanafi-Maturidi scholars. They held that a general expression may be uttered with the intention of a specific meaning. A delayed proof of specification (except a proof of qualification) is considered clarification (bayan) of what was intended from the beginning, not abrogation. They further held that a general form without any such proof is not itself proof that generality or absoluteness was intended. They considered the opposing view—that general forms always indicate generality and that a delayed specification is abrogation—to be the school of the Mu'tazila. The author then notes that in their own region, later scholars initially rejected those who held the opposing view and labeled them as Mu'tazilites. However, eventually a person emerged who inclined toward the Iraqi Hanafis in usul al-fiqh, followed them on this issue, and attributed the original Hanafi position to al-Shafi'i, while avoiding the issue of wa'id altogether. The author leaves his true belief to God.

Key Terms
عموم
خصوص
بيان
نسخ
اعتزال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think al-Maturidi rejected all general meanings or that he held that general expressions always intend specific meaning.
Correction: Al-Maturidi affirmed that a general expression can be intended for a specific meaning, but he did not say it is always the case. He held that a general form without a proof of specificity is not proof of intending generality, but it does not negate the possibility of general intention.

Checkpoint
According to al-Maturidi, what is the status of a delayed proof of specification (except qualification)?
A) It is abrogation (naskh).
B) It is clarification (bayan) of the intended meaning.
C) It is invalid.
D) It is evidence of ambiguity.

Key Takeaway
Al-Maturidi taught that a general expression may intend a specific meaning, and a delayed specification clarifies rather than abrogates.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a general command in the Quran, like 'O you who believe, fear Allah,' do you automatically assume it applies to you in every circumstance, or do you consider that it might be limited by other verses? This lesson shows that even scholars differed on how to interpret generality. What is your default assumption, and are you willing to seek clarification from the broader context before acting? Commit to one verse today where you will look for specific applications rather than assuming generality.
?
The passage records that some scholars accused others of being Mu'tazilites over this issue. Have you ever been quick to label someone's belief as deviant based on a secondary issue? Think of a time you did that. Can you, like the author, leave the final judgment to Allah and instead focus on understanding the person's reasoning? What step can you take to change that habit?
?
The author ends with 'And Allah knows best regarding his belief in that.' When you disagree with a scholar on a point of usul, do you feel the need to definitively categorize them, or can you hold your disagreement with humility? This lesson invites you to trust that Allah is the ultimate judge. Is there someone you disagree with whose belief you can entrust to Allah today? How would that change your attitude toward them?

The Salaf's Consensus on 'Umum and the Murji'ah's Rejection
Al-Nasafi recounts the salaf's consensus and Murji'ah's rejection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the historical background of the principle of 'umum (generality) in Quranic language, the unanimous stance of the Salaf, and why a sect from the Murji'ah rejected it.

Core Idea
The Murji'ah denied 'umum to avoid its theological implications for sinners.

This lesson begins by establishing that 'umum is a fundamental concept in Arabic linguistic understanding and was unanimously accepted by the Salaf. However, a sect of the Murji'ah later rejected it due to their doctrinal constraints. We'll examine their motivation and the historical context.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the Salaf's consensus
Read the first part of the passage where al-Nasafi states that 'umum is from Arabic understanding and was the doctrine of the Salaf with no disagreement. Notice the emphasis on unanimity.
2
Identify the Murji'ah's problem
Identify why the Murji'ah rejected 'umum: they felt constrained by their own doctrine of irja' and wanted to avoid the implication of eternal punishment for major sinners.
The key phrase: 'li'alla yalzamahum li-khusumihim al-qawl bi-wa'id al-fussaq'.
3
Reflect on the motive
Consider how theological commitments can lead to reinterpretation of clear linguistic principles. Ask yourself: is rejecting 'umum a valid approach?
This connects to the lesson's objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لصحة قوله فبان بما وصفنا أن العموم من مفهوم لسان العرب، وأن ذلك من مذهب السلف من غير خلاف بينهم وما خالف من هذا أحد من السلف ومن بعدهم إلى أن نشأت فرقة من المرجئة ضاق عليهم المذهب في القول بالإرجاء فلجأت إلى دفع القول بالعموم رأسًا لئلا يلزمها لخصومها القول بوعيد الفساق بظاهر الآي المقتضية لذلك. هذا كله لفظه ذكره في كتابه "المُصنف في أصول الفقه وقال أيضا في أول هذا الباب وحكى لي أبو الطيب بن شهاب عن أبي الحسن الكرخي أنه قال : إني أقف في عموم الأخبار وأقول بالعموم في الأمر والنهي، فقلت لأبي الطيب فهذا يدل على أن مذهبه كان الوقف في وعيد فساق أهل الملة، فقال : هكذا كان مذهبه، قال الجصاص وأبو الطيب هذا غير مهم عندي فيما يحكيه، وقد جالس أبا سعيد البردعي وشيوخنا القدماء ولم أسمع أن أبا الحسن الكرخي يفرق بين الخير والأمر والنهي في ذلك بل كان يقول بالعموم والإطلاق.
For the correctness of his statement, it became clear by what we described that generality ('umum) is from the understanding of the Arabic tongue, and that this was the doctrine of the salaf with no disagreement among them, and no one from the salaf or after them opposed this until a sect from the Murji'ah arose; the doctrine of irja' became constrained for them, so they resorted to rejecting the saying of generality entirely, lest their opponents necessitate for them the saying of threat (wa'id) against sinners by the apparent verses that require that. All this is his wording, he mentioned it in his book 'al-Musannaf fi Usul al-Fiqh.' He also said at the beginning of this chapter: Abu al-Tayyib ibn Shihab narrated to me from Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi that he said: 'I stop (waqf) regarding generality of reports, but I affirm generality in command and prohibition.' I said to Abu al-Tayyib: 'This indicates that his doctrine was waqf regarding the threat against sinners of the community.' He said: 'Such was his doctrine.' Al-Jassas and Abu al-Tayyib said: 'This is not important to me regarding what he narrates; he sat with Abu Sa'id al-Barda'i and our senior shaykhs, and I did not hear that Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi distinguished between the report and command and prohibition in that; rather, he used to affirm generality and absoluteness.'
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the historical position on 'umum (generality) in Quranic and prophetic language. Al-Nasafi begins by stating that 'umum is from the understanding of the Arabic language and that this was the unanimous position of the salaf. No one from the salaf or later scholars opposed this until a sect from the Murji'ah emerged; they found the doctrine of irja' (postponement of judgment) constraining, so they resorted to rejecting the principle of 'umum outright. Their motivation was to avoid the necessary implication that the verses threatening sinners apply generally, which would conflict with their view that major sinners are not eternally punished. Al-Nasafi then quotes from an earlier work, 'al-Musannaf fi Usul al-Fiqh,' where the author reports a narration from Abu al-Tayyib ibn Shihab from Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi. Al-Karkhi says: 'I stop (waqf) regarding generality of reports, but I affirm generality in command and prohibition.' The narrator then infers that this indicates al-Karkhi's position was waqf regarding the threat against sinners. Al-Karkhi's companion confirms this. However, al-Jassas and Abu al-Tayyib note that this distinction is not important; they point out that al-Karkhi studied under Abu Sa'id al-Barda'i and the early shaykhs, and they never heard him differentiate between report and command/prohibition; rather, he always affirmed generality and absoluteness. Thus, the passage defends al-Karkhi's consistency and highlights the controversy around 'umum in the context of wa'id.

Key Terms
العموم
الوعيد
المرجئة
الفساق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Murji'ah rejected 'umum because they doubted the Arabic language.
Correction: They rejected it due to theological constraints, not linguistic reasons.

Checkpoint
Why did the Murji'ah reject the principle of 'umum?
A) Because they denied the Arabic language
B) To avoid the implication that major sinners are subject to eternal punishment
C) Because they followed the Mu'tazila
D) Because they accepted 'umum only in commands and prohibitions

Key Takeaway
The principle of 'umum was universally accepted until a sect of Murji'ah rejected it to avoid theological implications for sinners.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Murji'ah twisted a linguistic principle to avoid facing uncomfortable truths from the Quran. In what area of your life have you bent Allah's clear words to fit what feels easier for you?
?
The Salaf were unanimous on 'umum, yet the Murji'ah broke away for theological convenience. Is there any matter of Islamic belief where you prefer the opinion of a group over the consensus of the Salaf simply because it suits your desires?
?
The Murji'ah were afraid that if 'umum applied to threat verses, they would have to admit that some sinners are doomed. Are you avoiding any clear warning from Allah because facing it would demand a painful change in your life?

Al-Karkhi's Stance on 'Umum and the Nature of Waqf
Al-Nasafi quotes al-Karkhi's position and the debate around it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi's position on 'umum, his distinction between reports and commands/prohibitions, and the scholarly evaluation of his stance.

Core Idea
Al-Karkhi practiced waqf on generality in reports but affirmed it in commands and prohibitions.

This lesson delves into the specific case of Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi, a prominent Hanafi jurist, who is reported to have stopped (waqf) regarding generality in reports but affirmed it in commands and prohibitions. However, later scholars like al-Jassas defended his consistency. We'll examine the narration and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Karkhi's statement
Read the quoted statement from al-Karkhi: 'I stop (waqf) regarding generality of reports, but I affirm generality in command and prohibition.' Notice the distinction.
2
Analyze the inference
The narrator infers that this means al-Karkhi also stopped regarding the threat (wa'id) against sinners. Consider if this inference is valid.
The phrase 'hada yadullu 'ala anna madhhabahu kana al-waqf fi wa'id fussaq ahl al-milla'.
3
Examine the scholarly response
Al-Jassas and Abu al-Tayyib rebut this inference, stating that al-Karkhi did not differentiate and always affirmed generality. Reflect on how scholars defend a colleague's consistency.
Key: 'lam asma' anna Aba al-Hasan al-Karkhi yufarriqu bayna al-khabar wa al-amr wa al-nahy'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لصحة قوله فبان بما وصفنا أن العموم من مفهوم لسان العرب، وأن ذلك من مذهب السلف من غير خلاف بينهم وما خالف من هذا أحد من السلف ومن بعدهم إلى أن نشأت فرقة من المرجئة ضاق عليهم المذهب في القول بالإرجاء فلجأت إلى دفع القول بالعموم رأسًا لئلا يلزمها لخصومها القول بوعيد الفساق بظاهر الآي المقتضية لذلك. هذا كله لفظه ذكره في كتابه "المُصنف في أصول الفقه وقال أيضا في أول هذا الباب وحكى لي أبو الطيب بن شهاب عن أبي الحسن الكرخي أنه قال : إني أقف في عموم الأخبار وأقول بالعموم في الأمر والنهي، فقلت لأبي الطيب فهذا يدل على أن مذهبه كان الوقف في وعيد فساق أهل الملة، فقال : هكذا كان مذهبه، قال الجصاص وأبو الطيب هذا غير مهم عندي فيما يحكيه، وقد جالس أبا سعيد البردعي وشيوخنا القدماء ولم أسمع أن أبا الحسن الكرخي يفرق بين الخير والأمر والنهي في ذلك بل كان يقول بالعموم والإطلاق.
For the correctness of his statement, it became clear by what we described that generality ('umum) is from the understanding of the Arabic tongue, and that this was the doctrine of the salaf with no disagreement among them, and no one from the salaf or after them opposed this until a sect from the Murji'ah arose; the doctrine of irja' became constrained for them, so they resorted to rejecting the saying of generality entirely, lest their opponents necessitate for them the saying of threat (wa'id) against sinners by the apparent verses that require that. All this is his wording, he mentioned it in his book 'al-Musannaf fi Usul al-Fiqh.' He also said at the beginning of this chapter: Abu al-Tayyib ibn Shihab narrated to me from Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi that he said: 'I stop (waqf) regarding generality of reports, but I affirm generality in command and prohibition.' I said to Abu al-Tayyib: 'This indicates that his doctrine was waqf regarding the threat against sinners of the community.' He said: 'Such was his doctrine.' Al-Jassas and Abu al-Tayyib said: 'This is not important to me regarding what he narrates; he sat with Abu Sa'id al-Barda'i and our senior shaykhs, and I did not hear that Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi distinguished between the report and command and prohibition in that; rather, he used to affirm generality and absoluteness.'
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the historical position on 'umum (generality) in Quranic and prophetic language. Al-Nasafi begins by stating that 'umum is from the understanding of the Arabic language and that this was the unanimous position of the salaf. No one from the salaf or later scholars opposed this until a sect from the Murji'ah emerged; they found the doctrine of irja' (postponement of judgment) constraining, so they resorted to rejecting the principle of 'umum outright. Their motivation was to avoid the necessary implication that the verses threatening sinners apply generally, which would conflict with their view that major sinners are not eternally punished. Al-Nasafi then quotes from an earlier work, 'al-Musannaf fi Usul al-Fiqh,' where the author reports a narration from Abu al-Tayyib ibn Shihab from Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi. Al-Karkhi says: 'I stop (waqf) regarding generality of reports, but I affirm generality in command and prohibition.' The narrator then infers that this indicates al-Karkhi's position was waqf regarding the threat against sinners. Al-Karkhi's companion confirms this. However, al-Jassas and Abu al-Tayyib note that this distinction is not important; they point out that al-Karkhi studied under Abu Sa'id al-Barda'i and the early shaykhs, and they never heard him differentiate between report and command/prohibition; rather, he always affirmed generality and absoluteness. Thus, the passage defends al-Karkhi's consistency and highlights the controversy around 'umum in the context of wa'id.

الوقف
الكرخي
الأمر
النهي
الخبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Karkhi actually made a distinction between reports and commands in 'umum.
Correction: According to al-Jassas, he always affirmed generality universally.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what did al-Karkhi reportedly say about 'umum?
A) He completely rejected 'umum
B) He affirmed 'umum in reports but stopped in commands
C) He stopped regarding reports but affirmed 'umum in commands and prohibitions
D) He accepted 'umum for everything except threats

Key Takeaway
Al-Karkhi's reported waqf in reports was disputed; his students maintained he consistently affirmed generality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a report about a scholar's position that contradicts their established teachings, do you accept it uncritically or do you seek confirmation from those closest to the scholar? How does this passage challenge you to be more rigorous in verifying claims about others, especially those you respect?
?
Al-Karkhi's students defended him against a report that could have damaged his legacy. Is there someone in your life whose character you know so intimately that you would defend them against a damaging report? What does that kind of loyalty require from you today?
?
The passage shows that a single reported statement can be overridden by the consistent testimony of direct students. Are there areas in your own life where your ingrained habits or principles are contradicted by a single action? How can you ensure that your consistent practice—not isolated incidents—defines who you are?

The Mu'tazila Dilemma on Promise and Threat Verses
Nasafi poses a dilemma to the Mu'tazila about a person with faith and major sins falling under promise verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma that Nasafi poses against the Mu'tazila regarding the application of general promise verses to believers who commit major sins.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila cannot consistently apply their principle of generality to both promise and threat verses.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi presents a powerful dialectical argument against the Mu'tazila. He forces them into a dilemma by showing that their own principle of generality, which they apply to threat verses, leads to a contradiction when applied to promise verses. This reveals an inconsistency in their theological framework.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage uses a series of conditional questions.
2
Identify the two types of verses
The passage refers to 'verses of promise' (آيات الوعد) and 'verses of threat' (آيات الوعيد). Understand that the Mu'tazila accept that both types are general in scope.
Example of promise verse: Quran 18:107 about Gardens of Paradise.
3
Understand the case of the major sinner
The person in question has both faith and good deeds but also commits major sins like consuming orphans' wealth or murder. Such a person is included in the general promise verses because of his faith and good deeds.
The Mu'tazila hold that major sinners will be eternally punished in Hell.
4
Trace the dilemma
If the Mu'tazila say this person enters Paradise, they abandon their doctrine of eternal punishment and accuse God of lying in threat verses. If they say he is eternally punished, they must either deny his inclusion in promise verses (abandoning generality) or accuse God of lying in promise verses. Both options lead to contradiction.
The argument then begins to discuss apostasy, but that is cut off in this passage.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider how this dilemma undermines the Mu'tazila position. Their own principle of generality forces them into a logical contradiction.
This shows the importance of a consistent hermeneutic for interpreting Quranic verses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل لهم: أليس أن بعض آيات الوعد وردت عامة، كما أن بعض آيات الوعيد وردت عامة ؟ فلا بد من بلى لوجود ذلك في نحو قوله تعالى: ﴿ إِنَّ الَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا وَعَمِلُوا الصَّالِحَتِ كَانَتْ لَهُمْ جَنَّتُ الْفِرْدَوْسِ نُزُلاً ﴾ وغير ذلك من الآيات. قيل: فإذا كان صاحب الكبيرة قد وجد منه الإيمان والأعمال الصالحة فهو من الذين آمنوا وعملوا الصالحات فصار كأنه ذكر باسمه الخاص وهو ممن قد أكل أموال اليتامى ظلمًا وصار كأنه ذكر باسمه الخاص، وكذا من قتل مسلمًا عمدًا ما حكمه؟ ومن أي قبيل هو أكان ممن يكون في جهنم خالدًا مخلدًا؟ أو ممن كانت له جَنَّتُ الْفِرْدَوْسِ نُزُلًا خَلِدِينَ فِيهَا لا يَبْغُونَ عَنْهَا حَوَلا فإن قال : هو ممن يدخل في الجنة فقد ترك مذهبه ووصف الله تعالى بالكذب حيث أخبر أنه يخلده في النار ولم يخلده. وإن قال: هو ممن يخلد في النار وهو مذهبه، قيل: أليس أنه كان داخلاً في قوله تعالى: كَانَتْ لَم جنَّتُ الْفِرْدَوْسِ نُزُلًا خَالِدِينَ فِيهَا ) فإن قال: لا. فقد ترك مذهبه في تناول صيغة العموم كل فرد من أفراد العموم بطريق التعيين وإن قال : نعم، فقد زعم أن الله تعالى كذب في الإخبار عنه أنه يخلده في الجنة.
ثم نقول : أليس أن من آمن وعمل الصالحات ثم ارتد عن الإسلام نعوذ
It was said to them: Is it not that some verses of promise came generally, just as some verses of threat came generally? So there is no escape from yes, because that exists in the like of His statement, the Exalted: 'Indeed, those who have believed and done righteous deeds, they will have the Gardens of Paradise as a lodging,' and other verses. It was said: So if a committer of a major sin has faith and righteous deeds from him, then he is among those who believed and did righteous deeds, so he becomes as if he was mentioned by his specific name, and he is among those who consumed the wealth of orphans unjustly, and he becomes as if he was mentioned by his specific name. Likewise, one who kills a Muslim intentionally, what is his ruling? And of which category is he? Is he among those who will be in Hell, eternally abiding? Or among those who have the Gardens of Paradise as a lodging, abiding therein forever, not seeking any change? If he says: He is among those who enter Paradise, then he has abandoned his doctrine and described God Almighty with lying, where He informed that He will eternally punish him in the Fire and did not. And if he says: He is among those who will be eternally punished in the Fire, and that is his doctrine, it is said: Is he not included in the statement of the Exalted: 'They will have the Gardens of Paradise as a lodging, abiding therein'? If he says: No, then he has abandoned his doctrine regarding the inclusion of the general form, i.e., every individual of the generality by way of specification. And if he says: Yes, then he has claimed that God Almighty lied in informing about him that He will eternally punish him in Paradise. Then we say: Is it not that one who believed and did righteous deeds then apostatized from Islam – we seek refuge
Source pages p. 407

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical argument against the Mu'tazila, using the principle of generality they themselves uphold. The author first establishes that there are general promise verses, just as there are general threat verses, using Surah al-Kahf 18:107 as an example. Then he presents a case: a person who commits major sins (like consuming orphans' wealth or killing a Muslim intentionally) but also has faith and good deeds. Such a person is included in the general promise verses because he has faith and good deeds. The author then poses a dilemma: (1) If the Mu'tazila say this person will enter Paradise, they abandon their own doctrine that major sinners are eternally in Hell, and they also accuse God of lying because God informed that such a person will be punished eternally (from threat verses). (2) If they say he will be eternally punished in Hell (their doctrine), they are asked whether he is included in the promise verse. If they say no, they abandon their principle that general forms include every individual. If they say yes, they accuse God of lying because God promised him Paradise but He would be punishing him in Hell. Thus, the Mu'tazila are forced into a contradiction. The argument then begins to discuss the case of apostasy, but it cuts off. This shows the inconsistency of the Mu'tazila position on generality.

Key Terms
آيات الوعد
آيات الوعيد
صاحب الكبيرة
العموم
الخلود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila can consistently apply generality to threat verses but not to promise verses.
Correction: This dilemma shows they cannot avoid contradiction: any answer leads to either abandoning their doctrine or accusing God of lying.

Checkpoint
What does the Mu'tazila have to say about a person who commits major sins but also has faith and good deeds, according to the dilemma?
A) He will definitely enter Paradise.
B) He will definitely be eternally punished in Hell.
C) He would be in a state between Paradise and Hell.
D) Either option leads to a contradiction with their own principles or God's truthfulness.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila are forced into a contradiction because they cannot consistently apply the principle of generality to both promise and threat verses without undermining God's truthfulness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief that you later realized could not be consistently applied to all relevant evidence, like the Mu'tazila here? What did you do when faced with that contradiction?
?
When you read general verses of promise or threat in the Quran, do you assume they apply to everyone except yourself? Or do you examine your own deeds with the same rigor you would apply to others?
?
If you were forced to choose between abandoning a cherished doctrine and accusing God of lying, which would you choose? What does that reveal about your attachment to truth over personal opinion?

Why a Mu'tazili or Khariji Major Sinner Becomes a Disbeliever
Nasafi gives two proofs that a Mu'tazili/Khariji major sinner becomes a kafir.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Nasafi's two-part argument that a Mu'tazili or Khariji who commits a major sin becomes a disbeliever due to despair and self-belief.

Core Idea
Committing a sin with wrong beliefs can make one a disbeliever by one's own logic.

Imam Nasafi addresses a specific sectarian issue: what if a Mu'tazili or Khariji commits a major sin? He argues that such a person becomes a disbeliever. His reasoning is twofold: first, despairing of God's mercy is a sign of disbelief, as the Quran states; second, if the person believes the sin makes him a kafir, then by committing it he believes himself a kafir, and that belief renders him truly a kafir. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic once, then the translation. Notice the two separate proofs: the Quranic verses and the logical reasoning.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Understand the first proof: despair
Identify how the Mu'tazili or Khariji despairs of God's mercy by committing a major sin, and how the Quran equates that with disbelief.
3
Understand the second proof: self-belief
Follow the logic: if he believes the sin makes him a kafir, then committing it leads him to believe he is a kafir, and that belief itself makes him a kafir.
This is a self-fulfilling logic based on his own creed.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this refutes the Mu'tazili/Khariji position: it turns their own doctrine against them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول : إن صاحب الكبيرة إذا كان معتزليًا أو خارجيًا يكفر لأنه بارتكابه ييأس من روح الله ويقنط من رحمة الله تعالى والله تعالى يقول: ﴿ إِنَّهُ لَا يَأَيْسُ مِن رَّوْحِ اللَّهِ إِلَّا الْقَوْمُ الكَفِرُونَ ) (١) وقال : ﴿ وَمَن يَقْنَطُ مِن رَّحْمَةِ رَبِّهِ إِلَّا الضَّالُونَ ﴾ (٢) : ولأنه لما ارتكب الكبيرة مع اعتقاد أنه يكفر أو يخرج عن الإيمان صار بارتكابه معتقدا أنه كافر أو خارج عن الإيمان، ومن اعتقد أنه ليس بمؤمن لا يكون مؤمنًا وكذا من اعتقد أنه كافر، والله الموفق (٣).
Then we say: Indeed the committer of a major sin, if he is a Mu'tazili or a Khariji, becomes a disbeliever because by committing it he despairs of the comfort of Allah and loses hope of the mercy of Allah Almighty, and Allah Almighty says: ‘Indeed, no one despairs of the comfort of Allah except the disbelieving people’ (12:87) and said: ‘And who loses hope of the mercy of his Lord except the astray?’ (15:56). And because when he committed the major sin with the belief that it makes him a disbeliever or takes him out of faith, he became by committing it a believer that he is a disbeliever or outside of faith. And whoever believes that he is not a believer is not a believer, and likewise whoever believes that he is a disbeliever. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 413

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that a major sinner who holds Mu'tazili or Khariji beliefs (that major sins cause eternal punishment or expulsion from faith) actually becomes a disbeliever by his own logic. The argument has two parts: First, by committing the sin he despairs of God's mercy, which is a characteristic of disbelievers as per the Quranic verses cited. Second, if he believes that committing a major sin makes him a kafir or takes him out of faith, then by committing it he believes himself to be a kafir or outside faith. Since belief determines one's state, he truly becomes a disbeliever. This is a refutation of the Mu'tazili and Khariji position that major sins entail eternal damnation or loss of faith.

Key Terms
معتزلي
خارجي
ييأس
يقنط
كفر
إيمان
اعتقاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That a Mu'tazili or Khariji major sinner remains a believer because they only deny eternal punishment, not faith itself.
Correction: The passage shows that by despairing of mercy and by the logic of their own belief (that sin causes disbelief), they actually become disbelievers.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does a Mu'tazili or Khariji who commits a major sin become a disbeliever?
A) Because he denies the prophets.
B) Because he despairs of God's mercy and believes himself to be a disbeliever.
C) Because he rejects the Quran.
D) Because he associates partners with God.

Key Takeaway
A Mu'tazili or Khariji's own creed, combined with committing a major sin, leads to their becoming disbelievers by despair and self-belief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever committed a sin and then felt a deep sense of hopelessness about Allah's mercy? According to this passage, what does that despair reveal about your faith? Will you repent with firm hope, or continue in despair that this passage equates with disbelief?
?
If you sincerely believed that a particular sin would take you out of the fold of Islam, and then you committed it, would you be honest enough to admit that by your own standard you have become a disbeliever? Or would you rationalize and still consider yourself a Muslim? This passage confronts you with the consistency of your beliefs and actions. Which is more real to you: what you claim to believe or what your actions show?
?
The Mu'tazili and Khariji are condemned here because their doctrine logically leads them to spiritual doom. Is there any belief you hold that, if followed to its conclusion, would harm your faith or lead you away from the truth? Are you willing to examine and correct it, even if it is uncomfortable?

Why Mu'tazili or Khariji Major Sinners Become Disbelievers
Nasafi argues that a Mu'tazili or Khariji major sinner becomes a disbeliever.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the reasoning behind the Maturidi position that a Mu'tazili or Khariji who commits a major sin becomes a disbeliever, based on their own theology and Quranic verses.

Core Idea
A sinner's own belief about sin shapes his faith status.

Nasafi presents a nuanced argument that not every major sinner becomes a disbeliever; rather, it is the combination of committing the sin and holding a specific theology about it that leads to disbelief. This passage focuses on the Mu'tazila and Khawarij, who believe that major sin removes faith, and shows how their own logic condemns them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the two distinct proofs: Quranic and logical.
2
Understand the Quranic proof
Nasafi cites two verses equating despair of God's mercy with disbelief. Since Mu'tazila and Khawarij believe major sinners are eternally punished, they effectively despair of mercy, meeting the Quranic criterion.
The verses are from Surah Yusuf and Surah al-Hijr.
3
Understand the logical proof
The second argument is based on self-assessment: if one believes that major sin removes faith, then committing it makes him hold that belief about himself, and that self-belief determines his status.
This argument relies on the consistency of their own doctrine.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this differs from the Ash'ari or Maturidi view that major sin does not remove faith. The conclusion applies only to those who already hold the belief that it does.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول : إن صاحب الكبيرة إذا كان معتزليًا أو خارجيًا يكفر لأنه بارتكابه ييأس من روح الله ويقنط من رحمة الله تعالى والله تعالى يقول: ﴿ إِنَّهُ لَا يَأَيْسُ مِن رَّوْحِ اللَّهِ إِلَّا الْقَوْمُ الْكَافِرُونَ ﴾ وقال: ﴿ وَمَن يَقْنَطُ مِن رَّحْمَةِ رَبِّهِ إِلَّا الضَّالُونَ ﴾ ولأنه لما ارتكب الكبيرة مع اعتقاد أنه يكفر أو يخرج عن الإيمان صار بارتكابه معتقدا أنه كافر أو خارج عن الإيمان، ومن اعتقد أنه ليس بمؤمن لا يكون مؤمنًا وكذا من اعتقد أنه كافر، والله الموفق.
Then we say: Indeed, the perpetrator of a major sin, if he is a Mu'tazili or a Khariji, becomes a disbeliever because by committing it he despairs of the spirit of God and loses hope in the mercy of God Almighty. And God Almighty says: "Indeed, none despairs of the spirit of God except the disbelieving people." And He said: "And who despairs of the mercy of his Lord except the astray?" And because when he committed the major sin with the belief that it makes him a disbeliever or takes him out of faith, he becomes by committing it believing that he is a disbeliever or outside of faith. And whoever believes that he is not a believer is not a believer, and likewise whoever believes that he is a disbeliever. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 413

Commentary
This passage is a proof that a Mu'tazili or Khariji who commits a major sin becomes a disbeliever. The argument proceeds in two steps. First, the author cites two Quranic verses: one stating that only disbelievers despair of God's spirit (Yusuf 87), and another stating that only the astray despair of His mercy (al-Hijr 56). The Mu'tazila and Khawarij, by asserting that major sinners are eternally punished, effectively despair of God's mercy for themselves, thus fulfilling the condition of disbelief. Second, the author argues that since these groups believe that committing a major sin removes one from faith, when one of them actually commits it, he is acting on that belief and thereby becomes a disbeliever. The conclusion is that such a person is not a believer because he himself believes he is not.

Key Terms
صاحب الكبيرة
معتزلي
خارجي
ييأس
قنوط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Committing a major sin always makes a person a disbeliever.
Correction: According to Nasafi, it is only when the person also holds the belief that major sin removes faith (as Mu'tazila and Khawarij do) that he becomes a disbeliever. The sin alone does not cause disbelief.

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider the Mu'tazili or Khariji major sinner a disbeliever?
A) Because he commits a major sin.
B) Because he believes the major sin makes him a disbeliever and thus he is acting on that belief.
C) Because he does not repent.
D) Because he rejects prophecy.

Key Takeaway
A Mu'tazili or Khariji becomes a disbeliever by committing a major sin because his own theology leads him to despair of God's mercy and to self-identify as a disbeliever.

?

Are you willing to examine whether that belief aligns with the Quran's emphasis on hope in God's mercy, as this passage highlights? How might you change your perspective to avoid falling into the same error as the Mu'tazila and Khawarij?
?

Mu'tazila Arguments Against Intercession
Nasafi presents the Mu'tazila arguments against intercession.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three Quranic proofs and rational arguments the Mu'tazila use to deny intercession for major sinners.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila argue intercession is only for the approved, and sinners are not approved.

The Mu'tazila take a strong stance against intercession for major sinners. In this passage, Nasafi presents their scriptural and rational arguments that aim to show that intercession cannot benefit those who commit major sins. This lesson clarifies their proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the structured presentation of arguments.
2
Identify the first premise
The passage begins with a premise: forgiveness without intercession is impossible for the major sinner, so intercession for him would be like forgiving a disbeliever. Understand this starting point.
3
Examine the three Quranic proofs
The Mu'tazila cite three verses: 21:28, 40:18, and 40:7-8. For each, note how they interpret the verse to exclude major sinners from intercession.
Pay attention to the word 'approval' and 'wrongdoers'.
4
Analyze the rational and practical arguments
After the Quranic proofs, they add rational arguments: divine wisdom cannot want good for enemies, the Prophet cannot love whom God hates, and an oath example. Follow each reasoning.
5
Consider the final claim
They conclude that affirming intercession for major sinners would embolden people to sin. Reflect on the logic of this argument.
This is a consequentialist argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعند المعتزلة لما كانت مغفرة صاحب الكبيرة بدون الشفاعة ممتنعة كان الشفاعة كمغفرة الكافر. ثم شبهتهم في ذلك قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَلَا يَشْفَعُونَ إِلَّا لِمَنِ ارْتَضَى ﴾ والفاسق الظالم ليس بمرضي، وقوله تعالى: ﴿ مَا لِلظَّالِمِينَ مِنْ حَمِيمٍ وَلَا شَفِيعٍ يُطَاعُ ﴾ وقاتل النفس ظالم، وقوله تعالى: ﴿ الَّذِينَ يَحْمِلُونَ الْعَرْشَ وَمَنْ حَوْلَهُ يُسَبِّحُونَ بِحَمْدِ رَبِّهِمْ وَيُؤْمِنُونَ بِهِ وَيَسْتَغْفِرُونَ لِلَّذِينَ آمَنُوا رَبَّنَا وَسِعْتَ كُلَّ شَيْءٍ رَحْمَةً وَعِلْمًا فَاغْفِرْ لِلَّذِينَ تَابُوا وَاتَّبَعُوا سَبِيلَكَ وَقِهِمْ عَذَابَ الْجَحِيمِ رَبَّنَا وَأَدْخِلْهُمْ جَنَّاتِ عَدْنٍ الَّتِي وَعَدْتَهُمْ وَمَنْ صَلَحَ مِنْ آبَائِهِمْ ﴾ الآية في الآية أنهم يسألون الجنة للمستحقين لها ولا يجوز غير هذا، إذ لا يجوز أن يقال: اجعل أعداءك أولياءك وأهل نارك أهل جنتك وافعل خلاف ما قلت وبدل حكمك مع ما يعلم أن الحكيم لا يريد الخير لأعدائه، ولا يجوز أن يكون النبي يحب من أبغضه الله تعالى ويوالي من عاداه. قالوا: ويحققه أنا نجد المسلمين والصالحين منهم يرغبون في شفاعة الرسول عليه السلام ويزهدون في أن يكونوا فساقا، ثبت أن الفساق لا شفاعة لهم. قالوا: والدليل على هذا أن من حلف بطلاق امرأته أنه يعمل عملاً يستوجب به الشفاعة أو ينال به الشفاعة يؤمر بالعمل الصالح ولا يؤمر بالفسق لأن في الأمر به خروج عن الإسلام دل ذلك أن الذي ينال الشفاعة هو المطيع دون العاصي. قالوا: ولأن في القول بإثبات الشفاعة لأهل الكبائر تجرئة للناس على الذنوب وهو باطل.
And according to the Mu'tazila, since forgiveness of the major sinner without intercession is impossible, intercession for him is like forgiveness of the disbeliever. Then their doubts in that are: His statement: "And they do not intercede except for whom He approves" and the sinful wrongdoer is not approved. And His statement: "For the wrongdoers there is no intimate friend nor any intercessor to be obeyed" and the murderer of a soul is a wrongdoer. And His statement: "Those who bear the Throne and those around it glorify the praise of their Lord and believe in Him and ask forgiveness for those who believe: 'Our Lord, You have encompassed all things in mercy and knowledge, so forgive those who have repented and followed Your way and protect them from the punishment of the Blazing Fire. Our Lord, admit them to the Gardens of Eden which You have promised them and whoever was righteous among their fathers...'" – the verse [indicates] that they ask for Paradise for those who deserve it, and anything else is not permissible. For it is not permissible to say: Make Your enemies Your friends and the inhabitants of Your Fire the inhabitants of Your Garden, and do the opposite of what You said and change Your judgment, especially since it is known that the wise one does not want good for His enemies. And it is not permissible for the Prophet to love whom God has hated and befriend whom He has opposed. They said: And it corroborates this that we find Muslims and the righteous among them desire the intercession of the Messenger (peace be upon him) and abstain from being sinners. It is established that sinners have no intercession. They said: And the proof of this is that one who swears by the divorce of his wife that he will do a deed that entitles him to intercession or obtains intercession is commanded to do righteous deeds and is not commanded to commit sin, because commanding it would be an exit from Islam. This indicates that the one who obtains intercession is the obedient one, not the disobedient. They said: And because affirmation of intercession for the perpetrators of major sins emboldens people to commit sins, and that is false.
Source pages p. 414

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila arguments against intercession for major sinners. It begins by stating their premise: forgiveness without intercession is impossible for the major sinner, so intercession for him would be like forgiving a disbeliever. Then they cite three Quranic proofs. First, verse 21:28 says intercession is only for those whom God approves, and the sinful wrongdoer (fasiq) is not approved. Second, verse 40:18 says wrongdoers have no intercessor, and the murderer is a wrongdoer. Third, verses 40:7-8 show that angels ask forgiveness only for those who believe and repent, implying that persistent sinners are excluded. They then add rational arguments: it would contradict divine wisdom for God to favor His enemies, and it would be impermissible for the Prophet to love whom God hates. They argue that people's desire for intercession while avoiding sin shows that only the obedient receive it. They give an example of an oath: if someone swears to do a deed earning intercession, he is commanded to do good, not sin. Finally, they claim affirming intercession for major sinners would embolden people to sin, which is false. Each proof is presented in sequence.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
الشفاعة
المغفرة
الفاسق
الظالم
ارتضى

Checkpoint
Which Quranic verse do the Mu'tazila use to argue that intercession is only for those whom God approves?
A) 21:28
B) 40:18
C) 40:7-8
D) 2:255

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila reject intercession for major sinners using Quranic verses and rational arguments, asserting that God's approval and the Prophet's love are reserved for the obedient.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Mu'tazila argue that affirming intercession for major sinners emboldens people to sin. Have you ever used the hope of intercession as an excuse to delay repentance? What is one sin you have been neglecting to address, and when will you turn to God about it?
?
The Mu'tazila say the Prophet can only love whom God loves. If you are persisting in a major sin, what does your relationship with the Prophet look like? Does it concern you that you might be among those he does not love? What will you change today to increase your love for him?
?
The Mu'tazila claim that only the obedient deserve intercession. While you may disagree with them, does this challenge you to examine your own obedience? Are you confident that you are among those who are 'approved'? If not, what step will you take this week to seek God's approval?

Refutation of Mu'tazila on Intercession: Verses of Approval and Wrongdoers
Refutes Mu'tazila interpretation of two intercession verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the Mu'tazila interpretation of the intercession verses by clarifying that every believer is approved by Allah and that the term 'wrongdoer' in the Quran refers specifically to disbelievers.

Core Idea
Intercession is for approved believers, not just sinless ones; 'wrongdoer' means disbeliever.

The Mu'tazila argue that intercession is only for those whom Allah approves, excluding grave sinners. They also cite the verse 'For the wrongdoers there is no intercessor' to deny intercession for sinful believers. In this passage, the author counters both arguments by showing that every believer, even if sinful, is approved by Allah, and that 'wrongdoer' in that context refers exclusively to disbelievers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the flow of the argument.
Notice how the author directly addresses the Mu'tazila claims.
2
Identify the first refutation
The first part addresses the verse 'They do not intercede except for whom He approves.' The author argues that every believer is approved because of their faith and good deeds, even if they commit grave sins.
3
Identify the second refutation
The second part addresses the verse 'For the wrongdoers there is no intercessor.' The author restricts this to disbelievers, who are absolute wrongdoers, not sinful believers.
This distinction is crucial for understanding the Sunni position on intercession.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرد على المعتزلة وتعلقهم بقوله تعالى: وَلَا يَشْفَعُونَ إِلَّا لِمَنِ ارْتَضَى قلنا: وكل مؤمن هو ممن ارتضاه الله تعالى وما معه من الإيمان والطاعات والحسنات المرضية عند الله تعالى لا يخرج من كونه مرضيا عند الله تعالى. وقيل: ولا يشفعون إلا لمن ارتضى الله أن يشفعوا له، فلم زعمتم أن الله تعالى لا يرضى بشفاعة صاحب الكبيرة وفيه الخلاف، وقوله تعالى: مَا لِلظَّالِمِينَ مِنْ حَمِيمٍ وَلَا شَفِيعٍ يُطَاعُ منصرف إلى الكافر إذ هو الظالم المطلق الذي لا عدل معه، فأما المؤمن الذي معه الإيمان والأعمال الصالحة فلا يسمى ظالمًا على الإطلاق، وما يزعمون أن الملائكة يسألون الجنة لأهل الجنة فهذا منه على مذهبه دعوى العبث على خيرة خلق الله تعالى في يوم الجمع الأكبر؛ لأن إدخال أهل الجنة واجب عندهم وحق مستحق لأهل ذلك وعلى هذا كان اشتغال الملائكة بسؤال ذلك إما لأنهم خافوا أن يظلم الله تعالى ويمنع أهلها من دخولها ويمنعهم حقهم الواجب عليهم، وإما إن اشتغلوا بالعبث فيقولون أعطهم ما أعطيتهم واغفر لمن غفرت له.
The refutation of the Mu'tazila and their adherence to His statement, the Exalted: 'And they do not intercede except for whom He approves.' We say: Every believer is among those whom Allah the Exalted approves, and what he has of faith, acts of obedience, and good deeds pleasing to Allah the Exalted does not remove him from being approved by Allah the Exalted. And it is said: 'And they do not intercede except for whom Allah approves that they intercede for him.' So why do you claim that Allah the Exalted does not approve intercession for the committer of a grave sin? And there is disagreement about it. And His statement, the Exalted: 'For the wrongdoers there is no intimate friend nor intercessor to be obeyed' refers to the disbeliever, for he is the absolute wrongdoer who has no equity with him. As for the believer who has faith and righteous deeds, he is not called a wrongdoer absolutely. And their claim that the angels ask for Paradise for the people of Paradise—this is, according to his school, a claim of futility against the best of Allah's creation on the Day of the Great Gathering, because according to them, admitting the people of Paradise to Paradise is obligatory and a due right for its people. On this basis, the angels' occupation with asking for that is either because they feared that Allah the Exalted would be unjust and prevent its people from entering it and deny them their due right, or they engage in futility, saying: 'Give them what You have given them and forgive those whom You have forgiven.'
Source pages p. 416

Commentary
This passage refutes the Mu'tazila interpretation of two verses concerning intercession. First, the verse 'They do not intercede except for whom He approves' (Quran 21:28): the author argues that every believer, due to his faith and good deeds, is approved by Allah, even if he commits grave sins. The Mu'tazila claim that Allah does not approve intercession for grave sinners is baseless because the believer's approval remains. Second, the verse 'For the wrongdoers there is no intimate friend nor intercessor to be obeyed' (Quran 40:18) applies only to disbelievers, who are absolute wrongdoers, not to sinful believers who have faith and righteous deeds. The passage then addresses the Mu'tazila argument that angels asking for Paradise for its people is futile, since according to the Mu'tazila, Paradise is obligatory for its people. The author points out that this either implies fear of injustice on Allah's part—which is impossible—or that angels engage in vain talk, both of which are absurd.

Key Terms
شفاعة
ارتضى
ظالم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'wrongdoer' in Quran 40:18 includes all sinners, including believers who commit grave sins.
Correction: The verse specifically refers to disbelievers, who are absolute wrongdoers. A believer with faith and good deeds is not called a wrongdoer absolutely.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why does the verse 'For the wrongdoers there is no intercessor' not apply to sinful believers?
A) Because sinful believers are not wrongdoers at all.
B) Because the verse refers specifically to disbelievers, who are absolute wrongdoers.
C) Because the verse is abrogated.
D) Because intercession is only for prophets.

Key Takeaway
Intercession is available for all believers, even grave sinners, because they are approved by Allah, and the term 'wrongdoer' in the relevant verse applies only to disbelievers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that 'every believer is among those whom Allah approves,' do you truly believe that includes you, with all your shortcomings and sins? What prevents you from feeling that approval in your daily life?
?
The Mu'tazila thought that only the sinless could receive intercession. Have you ever despaired of Allah's mercy because of your repeated sins? How does this lesson challenge that despair—and what does it demand you change in how you view your own failures?
?
If intercession is for believers even if they commit grave sins, does that make you complacent? Or does it deepen your gratitude and drive you to obey more? Examine your heart: which reaction is more real in you, and what will you do about it today?

Refutation of Mu'tazila Argument about Angels Asking for Paradise
Refutes Mu'tazila's claim that angels asking for Paradise is futile.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazila claim that angels asking for Paradise is futile is invalid, and how it necessarily leads to attributing injustice or futility to Allah.

Core Idea
Angels' supplication for Paradise is not futile; it would imply injustice or absurdity if it were.

The Mu'tazila argue that since Paradise is obligatory for its people, the angels' asking for it is an act of futility. In this passage, the author refutes this by showing that such a claim either implies that the angels feared Allah would be unjust (denying due reward) or that they engage in vain talk. Both are impossible, especially for the best of creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the logical argument presented against the Mu'tazila claim.
2
Understand the Mu'tazila premise
The Mu'tazila believe that admitting the people of Paradise to Paradise is an obligatory right for them. Therefore, they consider the angels' asking for it as futile.
3
Analyze the author's refutation
The author presents two possibilities: either the angels fear Allah's injustice (which is impossible), or they engage in futility (which is also impossible for the best of creation). Thus, the Mu'tazila claim is absurd.
Notice the use of reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرد على المعتزلة وتعلقهم بقوله تعالى: وَلَا يَشْفَعُونَ إِلَّا لِمَنِ ارْتَضَى قلنا: وكل مؤمن هو ممن ارتضاه الله تعالى وما معه من الإيمان والطاعات والحسنات المرضية عند الله تعالى لا يخرج من كونه مرضيا عند الله تعالى. وقيل: ولا يشفعون إلا لمن ارتضى الله أن يشفعوا له، فلم زعمتم أن الله تعالى لا يرضى بشفاعة صاحب الكبيرة وفيه الخلاف، وقوله تعالى: مَا لِلظَّالِمِينَ مِنْ حَمِيمٍ وَلَا شَفِيعٍ يُطَاعُ منصرف إلى الكافر إذ هو الظالم المطلق الذي لا عدل معه، فأما المؤمن الذي معه الإيمان والأعمال الصالحة فلا يسمى ظالمًا على الإطلاق، وما يزعمون أن الملائكة يسألون الجنة لأهل الجنة فهذا منه على مذهبه دعوى العبث على خيرة خلق الله تعالى في يوم الجمع الأكبر؛ لأن إدخال أهل الجنة واجب عندهم وحق مستحق لأهل ذلك وعلى هذا كان اشتغال الملائكة بسؤال ذلك إما لأنهم خافوا أن يظلم الله تعالى ويمنع أهلها من دخولها ويمنعهم حقهم الواجب عليهم، وإما إن اشتغلوا بالعبث فيقولون أعطهم ما أعطيتهم واغفر لمن غفرت له.
The refutation of the Mu'tazila and their adherence to His statement, the Exalted: 'And they do not intercede except for whom He approves.' We say: Every believer is among those whom Allah the Exalted approves, and what he has of faith, acts of obedience, and good deeds pleasing to Allah the Exalted does not remove him from being approved by Allah the Exalted. And it is said: 'And they do not intercede except for whom Allah approves that they intercede for him.' So why do you claim that Allah the Exalted does not approve intercession for the committer of a grave sin? And there is disagreement about it. And His statement, the Exalted: 'For the wrongdoers there is no intimate friend nor intercessor to be obeyed' refers to the disbeliever, for he is the absolute wrongdoer who has no equity with him. As for the believer who has faith and righteous deeds, he is not called a wrongdoer absolutely. And their claim that the angels ask for Paradise for the people of Paradise—this is, according to his school, a claim of futility against the best of Allah's creation on the Day of the Great Gathering, because according to them, admitting the people of Paradise to Paradise is obligatory and a due right for its people. On this basis, the angels' occupation with asking for that is either because they feared that Allah the Exalted would be unjust and prevent its people from entering it and deny them their due right, or they engage in futility, saying: 'Give them what You have given them and forgive those whom You have forgiven.'
Source pages p. 416

Commentary
This passage refutes the Mu'tazila interpretation of two verses concerning intercession. First, the verse 'They do not intercede except for whom He approves' (Quran 21:28): the author argues that every believer, due to his faith and good deeds, is approved by Allah, even if he commits grave sins. The Mu'tazila claim that Allah does not approve intercession for grave sinners is baseless because the believer's approval remains. Second, the verse 'For the wrongdoers there is no intimate friend nor intercessor to be obeyed' (Quran 40:18) applies only to disbelievers, who are absolute wrongdoers, not to sinful believers who have faith and righteous deeds. The passage then addresses the Mu'tazila argument that angels asking for Paradise for its people is futile, since according to the Mu'tazila, Paradise is obligatory for its people. The author points out that this either implies fear of injustice on Allah's part—which is impossible—or that angels engage in vain talk, both of which are absurd.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that angels asking for Paradise is indeed futile because Paradise is already guaranteed for its people.
Correction: The author shows that this claim either implies fear of Allah's injustice (impossible) or engagement in futility (also impossible for angels). Thus, the Mu'tazila argument is invalid.

Checkpoint
What does the author say is the implication of the Mu'tazila claim that angels asking for Paradise is futile?
A) It implies that angels are not aware of Allah's decrees.
B) It implies either fear of Allah's injustice or engagement in futility.
C) It implies that Paradise is not obligatory.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argument that angels' supplication for Paradise is futile is refuted by showing it leads to absurd consequences that cannot be attributed to Allah or His angels.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The angels, the best of creation, continue to ask for what is already guaranteed. When you make dua, do you truly believe it makes a difference, or do you sometimes feel it is pointless because Allah already knows? How will you change your perspective after this lesson?
?
If the angels' supplication were merely a formality, would you still engage in worship that feels repetitive? What does this passage teach you about the value of every act of worship, even those that seem redundant?
?
You have likely questioned why we pray for things Allah has already decreed. Based on this refutation, what false assumption about Allah's justice or your own role does that question reveal? What step will you take this week to make your duas more sincere?

Refuting Mu'tazila on Intercession: Repentance and Desire for Intercession
Refutes Mu'tazila by interpreting verse and addressing their objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Maturidi interpretation of Quranic verses on forgiveness supports intercession for grave sinners and refutes the Mu'tazila claim that desiring intercession implies approval of sin.

Core Idea
Desiring intercession is compatible with hating sin—just as desiring forgiveness is.

This passage continues the debate on intercession, addressing two key points: the correct interpretation of a Quranic verse about forgiveness, and the Mu'tazila objection that Muslims cannot both desire intercession and detest sin. The author shows that the desire for intercession is understood as a contingency for sin, parallel to the desire for forgiveness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then review the literal translation. Pay attention to the structure: the author first interprets a verse, then responds to an objection.
The passage is dense with argument; take your time to follow the logic.
2
Identify the two main arguments
Notice that the passage has two distinct parts: (1) an interpretation of the verse 'So forgive those who repented...' and a refutation of the Mu'tazila's misreading; (2) a response to the Mu'tazila objection about desiring intercession while hating sin.
The transition is marked by 'وقولهم: المسلمون يرغبون في الشفاعة...'
3
Analyze the analogy between forgiveness and intercession
The author argues that just as Muslims desire forgiveness while detesting sin, they also desire intercession as a safeguard against past or future sin. This refutes the Mu'tazila claim that desiring intercession implies approval of sin.
The key parallel is: desire for forgiveness does not mean approving sin; similarly, desire for intercession does not mean approving sin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله تعالى: فَأَغْفِرْ لِلَّذِينَ تَابُوا وَاتَّبَعُوا سَبِيلَكَ أي اغفر للذين تابوا عن الشرك بدليل قوله: واتبعوا سبيلك أي تابوا عن الكفر ولهم ذنوب اقترفوها وكبائر ارتكبوها في حالة الإيمان، فأما الصرف إلى ما صرفته المعتزلة فممتنع لما ذكرت وما قال إنه لا يجوز أن يقول الأخيار اللهم اجعل أعداءك وأهل نارك أهل جنتك وافعل خلاف ما قلت، ولا يجوز أن يجيب النبي عليه السلام من أبغضه الله ويوالي من عاداه الله هذا كله كلام بناه على أصله الفاسد، ومن الذي يسلم له أن المؤمن الذي زجى عمره في طاعة الله تعالى ومعه من الأعمال الصالحة ما لا يحصى يكون عدوا لله تعالى أو مبغضًا له، وفصل الخلف فيما قال قد مر الكلام فيه في المسألة المتقدمة. وقولهم: المسلمون يرغبون في الشفاعة ويزهدون في أن يكونوا فساقًا. قلنا: والمسلمون يرغبون في المغفرة ويزهدون في أن يكونوا مرتكبي صغائر، ثم نقول يرغبون في الشفاعة لو وجدت منه معصية فيما يستأنف من الوقت أو سبقت فيما مضى من الوقت ويتعوذون بالله تعالى من الفسق والمعصية كما قلتم أنتم في المغفرة. يحققه: أن رغبتهم في الشفاعة لا يمكن حملها على غير هذا الوجه فلا حاجة.
And His statement, the Exalted: 'So forgive those who repented and followed Your way' means forgive those who repented from shirk, as evidenced by His saying 'and followed Your way,' i.e., they repented from disbelief, and they have sins they committed and major sins they perpetrated while in a state of faith. As for turning it to what the Mu'tazila turned it to, it is impossible for what I mentioned. And what he said—that it is not permissible for the righteous to say 'O Allah, make Your enemies and the people of Your Fire the people of Your Paradise, and do contrary to what You said,' and it is not permissible for the Prophet, peace be upon him, to respond to one whom Allah hates and befriend one whom Allah opposes—all this speech is built on his corrupt principle. And who would concede to him that a believer who spent his life in obedience to Allah and has with him good deeds beyond count should be an enemy of Allah or hated by Him? And the detail of the disagreement in what he said has been discussed in the preceding issue. And their statement: 'Muslims desire intercession and detest being sinners.' We say: Muslims desire forgiveness and detest being perpetrators of minor sins. Then we say: They desire intercession if a sin occurs from him in the future time or preceded in the past time, and they seek refuge in Allah from sin and disobedience, just as you said regarding forgiveness. He confirms it: That their desire for intercession cannot be understood in any other way, so there is no need.
Source pages p. 416

Commentary
This passage continues the refutation of Mu'tazila on intercession, focusing on two points. First, the author interprets Quran 40:7 (or 40:3? Actually 'فَأَغْفِرْ لِلَّذِينَ تَابُوا وَاتَّبَعُوا سَبِيلَكَ' is from Quran 40:7) as referring to those who repented from shirk but still have sins (minor and major) committed while in a state of faith. This shows that forgiveness is for believers who have sins, contradicting the Mu'tazila view that grave sinners are excluded from intercession. The author dismisses the Mu'tazila reinterpretation as impossible. Second, the author addresses the Mu'tazila objection that Muslims cannot simultaneously desire intercession and detest being sinners. The response: Muslims also desire forgiveness while detesting sin; similarly, they desire intercession as a contingency for past or future sin, while seeking refuge from sin. This is parallel to their own view on forgiveness. The author concludes that the desire for intercession can only be understood in this way, so no further argument is needed.

Key Terms
توبة
شفاعة
فسق
مغفرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That desiring intercession implies approval of sin or that believers who desire intercession are indifferent to sin.
Correction: The author shows that Muslims desire intercession as a contingency for past or future sin, while simultaneously detesting sin and seeking refuge from it—exactly parallel to their desire for forgiveness.

Checkpoint
What does the author argue about the verse 'فَأَغْفِرْ لِلَّذِينَ تَابُوا وَاتَّبَعُوا سَبِيلَكَ'?
A) It refers to those who repented from shirk but still have sins.
B) It refers to those who repented from all sins completely.
C) It refers only to the Prophet and the angels.
D) It is abrogated and not applicable.

Key Takeaway
The desire for intercession is consistent with hating sin, and the Quranic verse supports forgiveness for believers with sins.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you feel comfortable relying on the hope of intercession without hating the sin itself? What would change today if you truly detested your sins as much as you desire forgiveness?
?
The Mu'tazila thought desiring intercession was hypocritical. Examine your own heart: is your hope for intercession a lazy comfort that lets you ignore your sins, or does it drive you to actively seek refuge from sin and increase good deeds?
?
If a lifetime of good deeds does not make a believer an enemy of Allah, why do you sometimes feel that your sins outweigh your worship? How can you shift your focus from despair to turning away from sin while still relying on Allah's mercy?

Refutation of the Mu'tazilite View on Forgiveness
Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that the grave sinner is not forgiven

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that the grave sinner is not forgiven by appealing to the Qur'anic promise of forgiveness for those who end their disbelief.

Core Idea
Forgiveness follows the end of disbelief, contradicting Mu'tazilite theology.

Nasafi has just established that iman is tasdiq. Now he turns to a sectarian objection: the Mu'tazilites hold that a grave sinner (fasiq) is not forgiven. In this short but decisive passage, he points to the Qur'anic verse that promises forgiveness to those who desist from disbelief. This refutes their position because it shows that ending kufr through iman merits forgiveness, whereas the Mu'tazilites deny forgiveness for grave sins.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic once, then the literal translation. Notice the logical structure: 'in it is a refutation' → 'for in the verse is a proof that...'
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite claim being refuted
The passage says 'according to them it is not so.' What is 'it'? The Mu'tazilites deny that the one who ends disbelief becomes deserving of forgiveness for their past sins. Understand that they hold that grave sinners are punished eternally if unrepentant.
The Mu'tazilite position is that a fasiq is neither believer nor disbeliever and will be punished forever if they die without repentance.
3
Connect the proof to the Qur'anic verse
The verse alluded to is 'If they desist, what is past will be forgiven them' (Quran 8:38). Nasafi argues that this verse proves that ending kufr (through iman) leads to forgiveness. Therefore, the person who ends kufr is deserving of forgiveness, contradicting the Mu'tazilite denial.
4
Reflect on the implication for iman
Since iman is defined as tasdiq (assent), the one who has tasdiq after kufr is forgiven. This reinforces that forgiveness is not earned by works but by the cessation of disbelief through true iman.
This lesson is part of a larger argument that iman alone suffices for salvation from eternal punishment, contrary to Mu'tazilite views on grave sins.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفيه إبطال قول المعتزلة فإن في الآية دليلاً أن ما ينتهي به الكفر تحصل به المغفرة ويكون من انتهى كفره أهلاً للمغفرة، وعندهم ليس كذلك.
And in it is a refutation of the Mu'tazilites, for in the verse is a proof that that by which disbelief ends obtains forgiveness, and the one whose disbelief has ended becomes deserving of forgiveness, but according to them it is not so.
Source pages p. 426

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer refuting the Mu'tazilite view. After establishing that iman is tasdiq, Nasafi points out that the verse 'If they desist, what is past will be forgiven them' implies that the person who ends his disbelief through iman becomes deserving of forgiveness. The Mu'tazilites, however, hold that a grave sinner (fasiq) is not forgiven; but here the person who ends kufr is forgiven, which contradicts their position.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
مغفرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A person who ends their disbelief by embracing iman is not automatically forgiven for past sins; they must also perform good works or avoid grave sins.
Correction: The Qur'anic promise ('If they desist, what is past will be forgiven them') indicates that ending disbelief itself merits forgiveness, without additional conditions.

Checkpoint
Why does the verse 'If they desist, what is past will be forgiven them' refute the Mu'tazilite view?
A) It shows that iman includes works
B) It shows that forgiveness is granted to those who end kufr by iman, unlike the Mu'tazilite view that the grave sinner is not forgiven
C) It shows that only the Mu'tazilite view is correct
D) It shows that desisting from kufr is impossible

Key Takeaway
Nasafi uses the Qur'anic promise of forgiveness for those who desist from disbelief to refute the Mu'tazilite claim that the grave sinner is not forgiven.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever despaired of God's mercy because of your sins, forgetting that He forgives even the worst disbelief? What sin in your life makes you feel furthest from forgiveness, and how does this verse challenge that feeling? Commit today to turning back to Him sincerely.
?
The Mu'tazilites restricted forgiveness; we may do the same by thinking certain sins are unforgivable. Is there a sin you've committed that you've convinced yourself is beyond God's mercy? How does the promise of forgiveness for ending disbelief give you hope? What step will you take this week to act on that hope?
